          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glacial_Lake_Agassiz_and_Glacial_Lake_Ojibway,_7,900_BPE.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Glacial lakes
         <a href="/wiki/Lake_Agassiz" title="Lake Agassiz">
          Agassiz
         </a>
         and
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial_Lake_Ojibway" title="Glacial Lake Ojibway">
          Ojibway
         </a>
         , 7,900 BPE.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       At the time of first European contact and later colonization,
       <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_peoples" title="Algonquian peoples">
        Algonquian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">
        Iroquois
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">
        Inuit
       </a>
       nations controlled what is now Quebec.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebb_Hodge191239_65-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebb_Hodge191239-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their lifestyles and cultures reflected the land on which they lived. Algonquians organized into seven political entities lived
       <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">
        nomadic
       </a>
       lives based on hunting, gathering, and fishing in the rugged terrain of the Canadian Shield (James Bay Cree,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innu_people" title="Innu people">
        Innu
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Algonquin_people" title="Algonquin people">
        Algonquins
       </a>
       ) and Appalachian Mountains (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mi%27kmaq_people" title="Mi'kmaq people">
        Mi'kmaq
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abenaki_people" title="Abenaki people">
        Abenaki
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/St._Lawrence_Iroquoians" title="St. Lawrence Iroquoians">
        St. Lawrence Iroquoians
       </a>
       , a branch of the Iroquois, lived more settled lives, growing corn, beans and squash in the fertile soils of the St. Lawrence Valley. They appear to have been later supplanted by the
       <a href="/wiki/Mohawk_people" title="Mohawk people">
        Mohawk
       </a>
       nation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cornelius1999_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cornelius1999-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Inuit continue to fish and hunt whale and
       <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">
        seal
       </a>
       in the harsh Arctic climate along the coasts of Hudson and Ungava Bay.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marsh1988_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marsh1988-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These people traded fur and food and sometimes warred with each other.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="New_France">
        New France
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">
        New France
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cartier.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1310" data-file-width="975" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Cartier.png/220px-Cartier.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Cartier.png/330px-Cartier.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Cartier.png/440px-Cartier.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cartier.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A depiction of
         <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Cartier" title="Jacques Cartier">
          Jacques Cartier
         </a>
         by
         <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9ophile_Hamel" title="Théophile Hamel">
          Théophile Hamel
         </a>
         , 1844. No contemporary likeness of Cartier has been found to exist.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
          <a href="#cite_note-69">
           [69]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Around 1522–1523, the Italian navigator
       <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_da_Verrazzano" title="Giovanni da Verrazzano">
        Giovanni da Verrazzano
       </a>
       persuaded
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Francis_I_of_France" title="King Francis I of France">
        King Francis I of France
       </a>
       to commission an expedition to find a western route to
       <a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">
        Cathay
       </a>
       (China). In 1534,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breton_people" title="Breton people">
        Breton
       </a>
       explorer
       <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Cartier" title="Jacques Cartier">
        Jacques Cartier
       </a>
       planted a cross in the
       <a href="/wiki/Gasp%C3%A9_Peninsula" title="Gaspé Peninsula">
        Gaspé Peninsula
       </a>
       and claimed the land in the name of King Francis I.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiendeau200736_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiendeau200736-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was the first province of New France. However, initial French attempts at settling the region met with failure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiendeau200736_70-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiendeau200736-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       French fishing fleets, however, continued to sail to the Atlantic coast and into the St. Lawrence River, making alliances with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aboriginal_people_of_Canada" title="Aboriginal people of Canada">
        First Nations
       </a>
       that would become important once France began to occupy the land.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PickettPickett2011_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PickettPickett2011-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_chiefs_of_the_Huron.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="1583" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Three_chiefs_of_the_Huron.jpg/220px-Three_chiefs_of_the_Huron.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Three_chiefs_of_the_Huron.jpg/330px-Three_chiefs_of_the_Huron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Three_chiefs_of_the_Huron.jpg/440px-Three_chiefs_of_the_Huron.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_chiefs_of_the_Huron.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Three Huron-Wyandot chiefs from
         <a href="/wiki/Wendake,_Quebec" title="Wendake, Quebec">
          Wendake
         </a>
         in Quebec. New France had largely peaceful relations with the indigenous people such as their allies the Huron. After the
         <a href="/wiki/Beaver_Wars" title="Beaver Wars">
          defeat of the Huron
         </a>
         by their mutual enemies the
         <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">
          Iroquois
         </a>
         many fled from Ontario to Quebec.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Samuel_de_Champlain" title="Samuel de Champlain">
        Samuel de Champlain
       </a>
       was part of a 1603 expedition from France that travelled into the St. Lawrence River.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Litalien2004_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Litalien2004-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1608, he returned as head of an exploration party and founded Quebec City with the intention of making the area part of the
       <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">
        French colonial empire
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrenon2000_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrenon2000-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiebel1999_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiebel1999-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBinot2004_75-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBinot2004-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Champlain's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Habitation_de_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Habitation de Québec">
         Habitation de Québec
        </a>
       </i>
       , built as a permanent
       <a href="/wiki/Fur_trade" title="Fur trade">
        fur trading
       </a>
       outpost, was where he would forge a trading, and ultimately a military alliance, with the
       <a href="/wiki/Algonquin_people" title="Algonquin people">
        Algonquin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wyandot_people" title="Wyandot people">
        Huron
       </a>
       nations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeaMilward2001_76-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeaMilward2001-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       First Nations traded their furs for many French goods such as metal objects, guns, alcohol, and clothing.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coureurs_des_bois" title="Coureurs des bois">
        Coureurs des bois
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Voyageurs" title="Voyageurs">
        voyageurs
       </a>
       and Catholic missionaries used river
       <a href="/wiki/Canoe" title="Canoe">
        canoes
       </a>
       to explore the interior of the North American continent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poulter2010_77-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Poulter2010-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They establishing fur trading forts on the
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">
        Great Lakes
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Br%C3%BBl%C3%A9" title="Étienne Brûlé">
        Étienne Brûlé
       </a>
       1615),
       <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">
        Hudson Bay
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Esprit_Radisson" title="Pierre-Esprit Radisson">
        Radisson
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9dard_des_Groseilliers" title="Médard des Groseilliers">
        Groseilliers
       </a>
       1659–60),
       <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">
        Ohio River
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">
        Mississippi River
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Cavelier_de_La_Salle" title="Robert Cavelier de La Salle">
        La Salle
       </a>
       1682), as well as the
       <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan_River" title="Saskatchewan River">
        Saskatchewan River
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Missouri_River" title="Missouri River">
        Missouri River
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pierre_Gaultier_de_Varennes_et_de_la_V%C3%A9rendrye" title="Pierre Gaultier de Varennes et de la Vérendrye">
        de la Verendrye
       </a>
       1734–1738).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chartrand2013b_78-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chartrand2013b-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After 1627, King
       <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIII_of_France" title="Louis XIII of France">
        Louis XIII of France
       </a>
       allowed the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Company_of_New_France" title="Company of New France">
        Company of New France
       </a>
       to introduced the
       <a href="/wiki/Seigneurial_system_of_New_France" title="Seigneurial system of New France">
        seigneurial system
       </a>
       and forbade settlement in
       <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">
        New France
       </a>
       by anyone other than
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">
        Roman Catholics
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris1984_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harris1984-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1629 there was the
       <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Quebec" title="Surrender of Quebec">
        surrender of Quebec
       </a>
       , without battle, to English
       <a href="/wiki/Privateer" title="Privateer">
        privateers
       </a>
       led by
       <a href="/wiki/David_Kirke" title="David Kirke">
        David Kirke
       </a>
       during the
       <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">
        Thirty Years' War
       </a>
       . However, Samuel de Champlain argued that the English seizing of the lands was illegal as the war had already ended; he worked to have the lands returned to France. As part of the ongoing negotiations of their exit from the
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-French_War_(1627%E2%80%931629)" title="Anglo-French War (1627–1629)">
        Anglo-French War
       </a>
       , in 1632 the English king Charles agreed to return the lands in exchange for
       <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIII_of_France" title="Louis XIII of France">
        Louis XIII
       </a>
       paying his wife's
       <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">
        dowry
       </a>
       . These terms were signed into law with the
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Saint-Germain-en-Laye_(1632)" title="Treaty of Saint-Germain-en-Laye (1632)">
        Treaty of Saint-Germain-en-Laye
       </a>
       . The lands in Quebec and
       <a href="/wiki/Acadia" title="Acadia">
        Acadia
       </a>
       were returned to the French
       <a href="/wiki/Company_of_One_Hundred_Associates" title="Company of One Hundred Associates">
        Company of One Hundred Associates
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       New France became a Royal Province in 1663 under King
       <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">
        Louis XIV of France
       </a>
       with a
       <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_Council_of_New_France" title="Sovereign Council of New France">
        Sovereign Council
       </a>
       that included
       <a href="/wiki/Intendant_of_New_France" title="Intendant of New France">
        intendant
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Jean_Talon" title="Jean Talon">
        Jean Talon
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayes2008_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hayes2008-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The population grew slowly under French rule,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preston2009_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Preston2009-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       thus remained relatively low as growth was largely achieved through natural births, rather than by immigration.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powell2009_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Powell2009-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To encourage population growth and to redress the severe imbalance between single men and women, King Louis XIV sponsored the passage of approximately 800 young French women (known as
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Les_filles_du_roi" title="Les filles du roi">
         les filles du roi
        </a>
       </i>
       ) to the colony. Most of the French were farmers ("
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadiens" title="Canadiens">
        Canadiens
       </a>
       " or "
       <a href="/wiki/Habitants" title="Habitants">
        Habitants
       </a>
       "), and the rate of population growth among the settlers themselves was very high.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIlwraithMuller2001_83-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McIlwraithMuller2001-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Seven_Years.27_War_and_capitulation_of_New_France">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Seven_Years'_War_and_capitulation_of_New_France">
        Seven Years' War and capitulation of New France
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">
        Seven Years' War
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Authorities in
       <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">
        New France
       </a>
       became more aggressive in their efforts to expel British traders and colonists from the
       <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Country" title="Ohio Country">
        Ohio Valley
       </a>
       . They began construction of a series of
       <a href="/wiki/Fortification" title="Fortification">
        fortifications
       </a>
       to protect the area.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1754,
       <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">
        George Washington
       </a>
       launched a surprise attack on a group of Canadien soldiers sleeping in the early morning hours. It came at a time when no declaration of war had been issued by either country. This frontier aggression known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jumonville_Glen" title="Battle of Jumonville Glen">
        Jumonville affair
       </a>
       set the stage for the
       <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">
        French and Indian War
       </a>
       (a US designation; in Canada it is usually referred to as the Seven Years' War, although
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Canadian" title="French Canadian">
        French Canadians
       </a>
       often call it
       <i>
        La guerre de la Conquête
       </i>
       ["The War of Conquest"]
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) in North America. By 1756, France and Britain were battling the
       <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">
        Seven Years' War
       </a>
       worldwide. In 1758, the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">
        British
       </a>
       mounted an attack on New France by sea and took the French fort at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louisbourg_(community)" title="Louisbourg (community)">
        Louisbourg
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       On September 13, 1759, the British forces of General
       <a href="/wiki/James_Wolfe" title="James Wolfe">
        James Wolfe
       </a>
       defeated those of French General
       <a href="/wiki/Louis-Joseph_de_Montcalm" title="Louis-Joseph de Montcalm">
        Louis-Joseph de Montcalm
       </a>
       on the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Plains_of_Abraham" title="Battle of the Plains of Abraham">
        Plains of Abraham
       </a>
       outside Quebec City. With the exception of the small islands of
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Pierre_and_Miquelon" title="Saint Pierre and Miquelon">
        Saint Pierre and Miquelon
       </a>
       , located off the coast of Newfoundland, France ceded its North American possessions to Great Britain through the
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">
        Treaty of Paris (1763)
       </a>
       in favour of gaining the island of
       <a href="/wiki/Guadeloupe" title="Guadeloupe">
        Guadeloupe
       </a>
       for its then-lucrative
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">
        sugar cane
       </a>
       industry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHunter1999505-506_87-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHunter1999505-506-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Royal_Proclamation_of_1763" title="British Royal Proclamation of 1763">
        British Royal Proclamation of 1763
       </a>
       renamed Canada (part of New France) as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Province_of_Quebec_(1763-1791)" title="Province of Quebec (1763-1791)">
        Province of Quebec
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Quebec_Act">
        Quebec Act
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Act" title="Quebec Act">
        Quebec Act
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Province_of_Quebec_1774.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="506" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Province_of_Quebec_1774.gif/220px-Province_of_Quebec_1774.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Province_of_Quebec_1774.gif/330px-Province_of_Quebec_1774.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Province_of_Quebec_1774.gif/440px-Province_of_Quebec_1774.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Province_of_Quebec_1774.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Province_of_Quebec_(1763-1791)" title="Province of Quebec (1763-1791)">
          Province of Quebec
         </a>
         in 1774
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       With unrest growing in the colonies to the south, which would one day grow into the
       <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">
        American Revolution
       </a>
       , the British were worried that the French-speaking Canadians might also support the growing rebellion. At that time, French-speaking Canadians formed the vast majority of the population of the province of Quebec (more than 99%) and British immigration was not going well. To secure the allegiance of the approximately 90,000 French-speaking Canadians to the British crown, first Governor
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Murray_(Quebec_governor)" title="James Murray (Quebec governor)">
        James Murray
       </a>
       and later Governor
       <a href="/wiki/Guy_Carleton,_1st_Baron_Dorchester" title="Guy Carleton, 1st Baron Dorchester">
        Guy Carleton
       </a>
       promoted the need for change. There was also a need to compromise between the conflicting demands of the French-speaking Canadian subjects and those of newly arrived British subjects. These efforts by the colonial governors eventually resulted in enactment of the
       <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Act" title="Quebec Act">
        Quebec Act
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of 1774.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Quebec Act provided the people of Quebec their first Charter of Rights and paved the way to later official recognition of the French language and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_culture" title="French culture">
        French culture
       </a>
       . The act also allowed
       <i>
        Canadiens
       </i>
       to maintain French
       <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">
        civil law
       </a>
       and sanctioned freedom of religion, allowing the Roman Catholic Church to remain, one of the first cases in history of state-sanctioned freedom of religious practice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_the_American_Revolution">
        Effects of the American Revolution
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Although the
       <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Act" title="Quebec Act">
        Quebec Act
       </a>
       was unrelated to the events in
       <a href="/wiki/Boston_Tea_Party" title="Boston Tea Party">
        Boston
       </a>
       of 1773, and was not regarded as one of the
       <a href="/wiki/Intolerable_Acts" title="Intolerable Acts">
        Coercive Acts
       </a>
       , the timing of its passage led British colonists to the south to believe that it was part of the program to punish them. The Quebec Act offended a variety of interest groups in the British colonies. Land speculators and settlers objected to the transfer of western lands previously claimed by the colonies to a non-representative government. Many feared the establishment of Catholicism in Quebec, and that the French Canadians were being courted to help oppress British Americans.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Invasion_of_Quebec.tif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="485" data-file-width="745" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ca/Invasion_of_Quebec.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Invasion_of_Quebec.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ca/Invasion_of_Quebec.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Invasion_of_Quebec.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ca/Invasion_of_Quebec.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Invasion_of_Quebec.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Invasion_of_Quebec.tif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Defending Quebec from an American attack during the
         <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Quebec_(1775)" title="Battle of Quebec (1775)">
          Battle of Quebec
         </a>
         in December 1775
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On June 27, 1775, General George Washington decided to attempt an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Canada_(1775)" title="Invasion of Canada (1775)">
        invasion of Canada
       </a>
       by the American
       <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">
        Continental Army
       </a>
       to wrest Quebec and the St. Lawrence River from the British. the invasion failed when British reinforcements came down the St. Lawrence in May 1776 and the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trois-Rivi%C3%A8res" title="Battle of Trois-Rivières">
        Battle of Trois-Rivières
       </a>
       turned into a disaster for the Americans. The army withdrew to Ticonderoga.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELefkowitz2008_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELefkowitz2008-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although some help was given to the Americans by the locals, Governor Carleton punished American sympathizers and public support of the American cause came to an end. In 1778,
       <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Haldimand" title="Frederick Haldimand">
        Frederick Haldimand
       </a>
       took over for
       <a href="/wiki/Guy_Carleton,_1st_Baron_Dorchester" title="Guy Carleton, 1st Baron Dorchester">
        Guy Carleton
       </a>
       as governor of Quebec.
      </p>
      <p>
       The arrival of 10,000 Loyalists at Quebec in 1784 destroyed the political balance that Haldimand (and Carleton before him) had worked so hard to achieve. The swelling numbers of English encouraged them to make greater demands for recognition with the colonial government.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-georgetown2_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-georgetown2-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To restore stability to his largest remaining North American colony, King George III sent Carleton back to Quebec to remedy the situation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W.J.Eccles_France_in_America_p.246_93-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-W.J.Eccles_France_in_America_p.246-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In ten years, Quebec had undergone a dramatic change. What worked for Carleton in 1774 was not likely to succeed in 1784. Specifically, there was no possibility of restoring the previous political balance – there were simply too many English people unwilling to reach a compromise with the 145,000 Canadiens or their colonial governor. The situation called for a more creative approach to problem solving.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W.J.Eccles_France_in_America_p.246_93-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-W.J.Eccles_France_in_America_p.246-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Separation_of_the_Province_of_Quebec">
        Separation of the Province of Quebec
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Plan_of_the_Inhabited_Part_of_the_Province_of_Quebec.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="596" data-file-width="800" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/A_Plan_of_the_Inhabited_Part_of_the_Province_of_Quebec.jpg/220px-A_Plan_of_the_Inhabited_Part_of_the_Province_of_Quebec.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/A_Plan_of_the_Inhabited_Part_of_the_Province_of_Quebec.jpg/330px-A_Plan_of_the_Inhabited_Part_of_the_Province_of_Quebec.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/A_Plan_of_the_Inhabited_Part_of_the_Province_of_Quebec.jpg/440px-A_Plan_of_the_Inhabited_Part_of_the_Province_of_Quebec.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Plan_of_the_Inhabited_Part_of_the_Province_of_Quebec.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A Plan of the Inhabited Part of the Province of Quebec, c. 1785 by James Peachey. Peter Winkworth Collection. Library and Archives Canada, e000756679
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Loyalists soon petitioned the government to be allowed to use the British legal system they were used to in the American colonies. The creation of Upper and Lower Canada in 1791 allowed most Loyalists to live under British laws and institutions, while the French-speaking population of Lower Canada could maintain their familiar French civil law and the Catholic religion.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Therefore, Governor Haldimand (at the suggestion of Carleton) drew Loyalists away from
       <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">
        Quebec City
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">
        Montreal
       </a>
       by offering free land on the northern shore of Lake Ontario to anyone willing to swear allegiance to George III. The Loyalists were thus given land grants of 200 acres (81 ha) per person. Basically, this approach was designed with the intent of keeping French and English as far apart as possible. Therefore, after the separation of the Province of Quebec,
       <a href="/wiki/Lower_Canada" title="Lower Canada">
        Lower Canada
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Canada" title="Upper Canada">
        Upper Canada
       </a>
       were formed, each with its own government.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W.J.Eccles_France_in_America_p.246_93-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-W.J.Eccles_France_in_America_p.246-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Patriotes.27_Rebellion_in_Lower_Canada">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Patriotes'_Rebellion_in_Lower_Canada">
        <i>
         Patriotes
        </i>
        <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
         '
        </span>
        Rebellion in Lower Canada
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Lower_Canada_Rebellion" title="Lower Canada Rebellion">
        Lower Canada Rebellion
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Incendie_Parlement_Montreal.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="615" data-file-width="922" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Incendie_Parlement_Montreal.jpg/220px-Incendie_Parlement_Montreal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Incendie_Parlement_Montreal.jpg/330px-Incendie_Parlement_Montreal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Incendie_Parlement_Montreal.jpg/440px-Incendie_Parlement_Montreal.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Incendie_Parlement_Montreal.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The burning of the Parliament Buildings in Montreal occurred on the night of April 25, 1849.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1837, residents of Lower Canada – led by
       <a href="/wiki/Louis-Joseph_Papineau" title="Louis-Joseph Papineau">
        Louis-Joseph Papineau
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nelson_(insurrectionist)" title="Robert Nelson (insurrectionist)">
        Robert Nelson
       </a>
       – formed an armed resistance group to seek an end to the unilateral control of the British governors.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They made a Declaration of Rights with equality for all citizens without discrimination and a Declaration of Independence of Lower-Canada in 1838.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their actions resulted in rebellions in both Lower and
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Canada" title="Upper Canada">
        Upper Canada
       </a>
       . An unprepared
       <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">
        British Army
       </a>
       had to raise
       <a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">
        militia
       </a>
       force; the rebel forces scored a victory in
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Saint-Denis_(1837)" title="Battle of Saint-Denis (1837)">
        Saint-Denis
       </a>
       but were soon defeated.
      </p>
      <p>
       After the rebellions,
       <a href="/wiki/John_Lambton,_1st_Earl_of_Durham" title="John Lambton, 1st Earl of Durham">
        Lord Durham
       </a>
       was asked to undertake a study and prepare a
       <a href="/wiki/Report_on_the_Affairs_of_British_North_America" title="Report on the Affairs of British North America">
        report
       </a>
       on the matter and to offer a solution for the British Parliament to assess.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-durham_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-durham-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Following Durham's report,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-durham_97-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-durham-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the British government merged the two colonial provinces into one
       <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Canada" title="Province of Canada">
        Province of Canada
       </a>
       in 1840 with the
       <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1840" title="Act of Union 1840">
        Act of Union
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-actunion_98-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-actunion-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The two colonies remained distinct in administration, election, and law.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1848, Baldwin and LaFontaine, allies and leaders of the Reformist party, were asked by
       <a href="/wiki/James_Bruce,_8th_Earl_of_Elgin" title="James Bruce, 8th Earl of Elgin">
        Lord Elgin
       </a>
       to form an administration together under the new policy of
       <a href="/wiki/Responsible_government" title="Responsible government">
        responsible government
       </a>
       . The French language subsequently regained legal status in the Legislature.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-actunion_98-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-actunion-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Canadian_Confederation">
        Canadian Confederation
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Confederation" title="Canadian Confederation">
        Canadian Confederation
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the 1860s, the delegates from the colonies of
       <a href="/wiki/British_North_America" title="British North America">
        British North America
       </a>
       (Canada, New Brunswick, Nova Scotia, Prince Edward Island, and Newfoundland) met in a series of conferences to discuss self-governing status for a new confederation. The first
       <a href="/wiki/Charlottetown_Conference" title="Charlottetown Conference">
        Charlottetown Conference
       </a>
       took place in
       <a href="/wiki/Charlottetown" title="Charlottetown">
        Charlottetown
       </a>
       , Prince Edward Island, followed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Conference,_1864" title="Quebec Conference, 1864">
        Quebec Conference
       </a>
       in Quebec City which led to a delegation going to London, Britain, to put forth a proposal for a national union.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As a result of those deliberations, in 1867 the
       <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">
        Parliament of the United Kingdom
       </a>
       passed the
       <a href="/wiki/British_North_America_Acts" title="British North America Acts">
        British North America Acts
       </a>
       , providing for the Confederation of most of these provinces. The former
       <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Canada" title="Province of Canada">
        Province of Canada
       </a>
       was divided into its two previous parts as the provinces of
       <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">
        Ontario
       </a>
       (Upper Canada) and Quebec (Lower Canada).
       <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">
        New Brunswick
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">
        Nova Scotia
       </a>
       joined Ontario and Quebec in the new
       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
        Dominion of Canada
       </a>
       . The other provinces then joined the Confederation, one after the other:
       <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">
        Manitoba
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">
        Northwest Territories
       </a>
       in 1870,
       <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">
        British Columbia
       </a>
       in 1871,
       <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">
        Prince Edward Island
       </a>
       in 1873,
       <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">
        Yukon
       </a>
       in 1898,
       <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">
        Alberta
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">
        Saskatchewan
       </a>
       in 1905,
       <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">
        Newfoundland
       </a>
       in 1949 and finally
       <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">
        Nunavut
       </a>
       in 1999.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Winston_Churchill_at_a_conference_in_Quebec_-_NARA_-_197118.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2376" data-file-width="3000" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Winston_Churchill_at_a_conference_in_Quebec_-_NARA_-_197118.jpg/220px-Winston_Churchill_at_a_conference_in_Quebec_-_NARA_-_197118.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Winston_Churchill_at_a_conference_in_Quebec_-_NARA_-_197118.jpg/330px-Winston_Churchill_at_a_conference_in_Quebec_-_NARA_-_197118.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Winston_Churchill_at_a_conference_in_Quebec_-_NARA_-_197118.jpg/440px-Winston_Churchill_at_a_conference_in_Quebec_-_NARA_-_197118.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Winston_Churchill_at_a_conference_in_Quebec_-_NARA_-_197118.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">
          Winston Churchill
         </a>
         in Québec City in 1943
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I_and_World_War_II">
        World War I and World War II
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       When the United Kingdom declared war on August 4, 1914, Canada was automatically involved as a dominion. About 6,000 volunteers from Quebec participated on the European front. Although reaction to conscription was favourable in English Canada the idea was deeply unpopular in Quebec. The
       <a href="/wiki/Conscription_Crisis_of_1917" title="Conscription Crisis of 1917">
        Conscription Crisis of 1917
       </a>
       did much to highlight the divisions between French and English-speaking Canadians in Canada.
      </p>
      <p>
       During
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       , the participation of Quebec was more important but led to the
       <a href="/wiki/Conscription_Crisis_of_1944" title="Conscription Crisis of 1944">
        Conscription Crisis of 1944
       </a>
       and opposition. Many Quebecers fought against the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_power" title="Axis power">
        axis power
       </a>
       between 1939 to 1945 with the involvement of many francophone regiments such as
       <a href="/wiki/Les_Fusiliers_Mont-Royal" title="Les Fusiliers Mont-Royal">
        Les Fusiliers Mont-Royal
       </a>
       , le
       <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9giment_de_la_Chaudi%C3%A8re" title="Régiment de la Chaudière">
        Régiment de la Chaudière
       </a>
       and many more.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Quiet_Revolution">
        Quiet Revolution
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Quiet_Revolution" title="Quiet Revolution">
        Quiet Revolution
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Godbout_speech.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="327" data-file-width="235" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Godbout_speech.jpg/170px-Godbout_speech.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Godbout_speech.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Godbout_speech.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Ad%C3%A9lard_Godbout" title="Adélard Godbout">
          Adélard Godbout
         </a>
         implemented a program of progressive legislation that laid the groundwork for the Quiet Revolution
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The conservative government of
       <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Duplessis" title="Maurice Duplessis">
        Maurice Duplessis
       </a>
       and his
       <a href="/wiki/Union_Nationale_(Quebec)" title="Union Nationale (Quebec)">
        Union Nationale
       </a>
       dominated Quebec politics from 1944 to 1959 with the support of the Catholic Church.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Trudeau" title="Pierre Trudeau">
        Pierre Trudeau
       </a>
       and other liberals formed an intellectual opposition to Duplessis's regime, setting the groundwork for the
       <a href="/wiki/Quiet_Revolution" title="Quiet Revolution">
        Quiet Revolution
       </a>
       under
       <a href="/wiki/Jean_Lesage" title="Jean Lesage">
        Jean Lesage
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Liberal_Party" title="Quebec Liberal Party">
        Liberals
       </a>
       . The Quiet Revolution was a period of dramatic social and political change
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-short_history_102-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-short_history-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       that saw the decline of Anglo supremacy in the Quebec economy, the decline of the Roman Catholic Church's influence,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-georgetown1_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-georgetown1-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the formation of
       <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">
        hydroelectric
       </a>
       companies under
       <a href="/wiki/Hydro-Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Hydro-Québec">
        Hydro-Québec
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-short_history_102-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-short_history-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the emergence of a
       <a href="/wiki/Quebec_sovereignty_movement" title="Quebec sovereignty movement">
        pro-sovereignty movement
       </a>
       under former Liberal minister
       <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_L%C3%A9vesque" title="René Lévesque">
        René Lévesque
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="October_Crisis">
        October Crisis
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/October_Crisis" title="October Crisis">
        October Crisis
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Front_de_lib%C3%A9ration_du_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Front de libération du Québec">
        Front de libération du Québec
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Beginning in 1963, a paramilitary group that became known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Front_de_lib%C3%A9ration_du_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Front de libération du Québec">
        Front de libération du Québec
       </a>
       (FLQ) launched a decade-long series of propaganda and terrorism that included bombings, robberies and attacks
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FLQ_103-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FLQ-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       directed primarily at English institutions, resulting in at least five deaths. In 1970, their activities culminated in events referred to as the
       <a href="/wiki/October_Crisis" title="October Crisis">
        October Crisis
       </a>
       when
       <a href="/wiki/James_Cross" title="James Cross">
        James Cross
       </a>
       , the British trade commissioner to Canada, was kidnapped along with
       <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Laporte" title="Pierre Laporte">
        Pierre Laporte
       </a>
       , a provincial minister and Vice-Premier.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tetley2006AppendixD_104-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tetley2006AppendixD-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Laporte was strangled with his own rosary beads a few days later. In their published Manifesto, the militants stated: "In the coming year
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bourassa" title="Robert Bourassa">
        Bourassa
       </a>
       will have to face reality; 100,000 revolutionary workers, armed and organized." At the request of Premier Robert Bourassa, Prime Minister
       <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Trudeau" title="Pierre Trudeau">
        Pierre Trudeau
       </a>
       invoked the
       <a href="/wiki/War_Measures_Act" title="War Measures Act">
        War Measures Act
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Parti_Qu.C3.A9b.C3.A9cois_and_national_unity">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Parti_Québécois_and_national_unity">
        Parti Québécois and national unity
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Parti_Qu%C3%A9b%C3%A9cois" title="Parti Québécois">
        Parti Québécois
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Quebec_sovereignty_movement" title="Quebec sovereignty movement">
        Quebec sovereignty movement
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1977, the newly elected
       <a href="/wiki/Parti_Qu%C3%A9b%C3%A9cois" title="Parti Québécois">
        Parti Québécois
       </a>
       government of
       <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_L%C3%A9vesque" title="René Lévesque">
        René Lévesque
       </a>
       introduced the
       <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_the_French_Language" title="Charter of the French Language">
        Charter of the French Language
       </a>
       . Often known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_101" title="Bill 101">
        Bill 101
       </a>
       , it defined French as the only official language of Quebec in areas of provincial jurisdiction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Lévesque and his party had run in the 1970 and 1973 Quebec elections under a platform of separating Quebec from the rest of Canada. The party failed to win control of Quebec's National Assembly both times – though its share of the vote increased from 23 percent to 30 percent – and Lévesque was defeated both times in the
       <a href="/wiki/Electoral_district_(Canada)" title="Electoral district (Canada)">
        riding
       </a>
       he contested.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 1976 election, he softened his message by promising a referendum (plebiscite) on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sovereignty-association" title="Sovereignty-association">
        sovereignty-association
       </a>
       rather than outright separation, by which Quebec would have independence in most government functions but share some other ones, such as a common currency, with Canada. On November 15, 1976, Lévesque and the Parti Québécois won control of the provincial government for the first time. The question of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sovereignty-association" title="Sovereignty-association">
        sovereignty-association
       </a>
       was placed before the voters in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1980_Quebec_referendum" title="1980 Quebec referendum">
        1980 Quebec referendum
       </a>
       . During the campaign,
       <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Trudeau" title="Pierre Trudeau">
        Pierre Trudeau
       </a>
       promised that a vote for the "no" side was a vote for reforming Canada. Trudeau advocated the
       <a href="/wiki/Patriation" title="Patriation">
        patriation
       </a>
       of Canada's Constitution from the United Kingdom. The existing constitutional document, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_North_America_Act" title="British North America Act">
        British North America Act
       </a>
       , could only be amended by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Parliament" title="United Kingdom Parliament">
        United Kingdom Parliament
       </a>
       upon a request by the Canadian parliament.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rene_Levesque_-_election_1973_-_LAC_PA115039.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="258" data-file-width="386" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/76/Rene_Levesque_-_election_1973_-_LAC_PA115039.jpg/220px-Rene_Levesque_-_election_1973_-_LAC_PA115039.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/76/Rene_Levesque_-_election_1973_-_LAC_PA115039.jpg/330px-Rene_Levesque_-_election_1973_-_LAC_PA115039.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/76/Rene_Levesque_-_election_1973_-_LAC_PA115039.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rene_Levesque_-_election_1973_-_LAC_PA115039.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_L%C3%A9vesque" title="René Lévesque">
          René Lévesque
         </a>
         in Paul Sauvé arena, Montreal, on the 1973 election night
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Sixty percent of the Quebec electorate voted against the proposition for sovereignty-association.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Polls showed that the overwhelming majority of English and immigrant Quebecers voted against, and that French Quebecers were almost equally divided, with older voters less in favour and younger voters more in favour. After his loss in the referendum, Lévesque went back to Ottawa to start negotiating a new constitution with Trudeau, his minister of Justice
       <a href="/wiki/Jean_Chr%C3%A9tien" title="Jean Chrétien">
        Jean Chrétien
       </a>
       and the nine other provincial premiers. Lévesque insisted Quebec be able to veto any future constitutional amendments. The negotiations quickly reached a stand-still. Quebec is the only province not to have assented to the
       <a href="/wiki/Patriation" title="Patriation">
        patriation
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Canada" title="Constitution of Canada">
        Canadian constitution
       </a>
       in 1982.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
        <a href="#cite_note-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In subsequent years, two attempts were made to gain Quebec's approval of the constitution. The first was the
       <a href="/wiki/Meech_Lake_Accord" title="Meech Lake Accord">
        Meech Lake Accord
       </a>
       of 1987, which was finally abandoned in 1990 when the province of
       <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">
        Manitoba
       </a>
       did not pass it within the established deadline. (
       <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">
        Newfoundland
       </a>
       premier
       <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Wells" title="Clyde Wells">
        Clyde Wells
       </a>
       had expressed his opposition to the accord, but, with the failure in Manitoba, the vote for or against Meech never took place in his province.) This led to the formation of the sovereigntist
       <a href="/wiki/Bloc_Qu%C3%A9b%C3%A9cois" title="Bloc Québécois">
        Bloc Québécois
       </a>
       party in Ottawa under the leadership of
       <a href="/wiki/Lucien_Bouchard" title="Lucien Bouchard">
        Lucien Bouchard
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
       who had resigned from the federal cabinet. The second attempt, the
       <a href="/wiki/Charlottetown_Accord" title="Charlottetown Accord">
        Charlottetown Accord
       </a>
       of 1992, also failed to gain traction. This result caused a split in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parti_lib%C3%A9ral_du_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Parti libéral du Québec">
        Quebec Liberal Party
       </a>
       that led to the formation of the new
       <a href="/wiki/Action_d%C3%A9mocratique_du_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Action démocratique du Québec">
        Action démocratique
       </a>
       (Democratic Action) party led by
       <a href="/wiki/Mario_Dumont" title="Mario Dumont">
        Mario Dumont
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Jean_Allaire" title="Jean Allaire">
        Jean Allaire
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:R%C3%A9f%C3%A9rendum_1995.PNG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="826" data-file-width="1129" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/R%C3%A9f%C3%A9rendum_1995.PNG/220px-R%C3%A9f%C3%A9rendum_1995.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/R%C3%A9f%C3%A9rendum_1995.PNG/330px-R%C3%A9f%C3%A9rendum_1995.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/R%C3%A9f%C3%A9rendum_1995.PNG/440px-R%C3%A9f%C3%A9rendum_1995.PNG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:R%C3%A9f%C3%A9rendum_1995.PNG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The results of the 1995 Quebec referendum per circonscription. Dark brown means high no %; dark blue means high yes %
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On October 30, 1995, with the
       <a href="/wiki/Parti_Qu%C3%A9b%C3%A9cois" title="Parti Québécois">
        Parti Québécois
       </a>
       back in power since 1994, a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1995_Quebec_referendum" title="1995 Quebec referendum">
        second referendum
       </a>
       on sovereignty took place. This time, it was rejected by a slim majority (50.6 percent NO to 49.4 percent YES).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1995_facts_110-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-1995_facts-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Statut_particulier_.28.22special_status.22.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='Statut_particulier_("special_status")'>
        <i>
         Statut particulier
        </i>
        ("special status")
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Given the province's heritage and the preponderance of French (unique among the Canadian provinces), there has been debate in Canada regarding the unique status (
       <i>
        statut particulier
       </i>
       ) of Quebec and its people, wholly or partially. Prior attempts to amend the Canadian constitution to acknowledge Quebec as a "
       <a href="/wiki/Distinct_society" title="Distinct society">
        distinct society
       </a>
       " – referring to the province's uniqueness within Canada regarding law, language, and culture – have been unsuccessful; however, the federal government under Prime Minister
       <a href="/wiki/Jean_Chr%C3%A9tien" title="Jean Chrétien">
        Jean Chrétien
       </a>
       would later endorse recognition of Quebec as a distinct society.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On October 30, 2003, the National Assembly of Quebec voted unanimously to affirm "that the people of Québec form a nation."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On November 27, 2006, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_House_of_Commons" title="Canadian House of Commons">
        House of Commons
       </a>
       passed a
       <a href="/wiki/Qu%C3%A9b%C3%A9cois_nation_motion" title="Québécois nation motion">
        symbolic motion
       </a>
       moved by Prime Minister
       <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Harper" title="Stephen Harper">
        Stephen Harper
       </a>
       declaring "that this House recognize that the
       <a href="/wiki/French-speaking_Quebecer" title="French-speaking Quebecer">
        Québécois
       </a>
       form a nation within a united Canada."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
        <a href="#cite_note-113">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
        <a href="#cite_note-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, there is considerable debate and uncertainty over what this means.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">
        Government and politics
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Quebec" title="Politics of Quebec">
        Politics of Quebec
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_in_Quebec" title="Monarchy in Quebec">
        Monarchy in Quebec
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Quebec" title="Government of Quebec">
        Government of Quebec
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qu%C3%A9bec_-_H%C3%B4tel_du_Parlement_3.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1623" data-file-width="2587" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Qu%C3%A9bec_-_H%C3%B4tel_du_Parlement_3.jpg/220px-Qu%C3%A9bec_-_H%C3%B4tel_du_Parlement_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Qu%C3%A9bec_-_H%C3%B4tel_du_Parlement_3.jpg/330px-Qu%C3%A9bec_-_H%C3%B4tel_du_Parlement_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Qu%C3%A9bec_-_H%C3%B4tel_du_Parlement_3.jpg/440px-Qu%C3%A9bec_-_H%C3%B4tel_du_Parlement_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qu%C3%A9bec_-_H%C3%B4tel_du_Parlement_3.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Building_(Quebec)" title="Parliament Building (Quebec)">
          Parliament Building
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">
          Quebec City
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Governor_of_Quebec" title="Lieutenant Governor of Quebec">
        Lieutenant Governor
       </a>
       represents the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_of_Canada" title="Queen of Canada">
        Queen of Canada
       </a>
       and acts as the province's
       <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">
        head of state
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
        <a href="#cite_note-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The head of government is the
       <a href="/wiki/Premier_of_Quebec" title="Premier of Quebec">
        premier
       </a>
       (called
       <i>
        premier ministre
       </i>
       in French) who leads the largest party in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicameral" title="Unicameral">
        unicameral
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Quebec" title="National Assembly of Quebec">
        National Assembly
       </a>
       , or
       <i>
        Assemblée Nationale
       </i>
       , from which the
       <a href="/wiki/Executive_Council_of_Quebec" title="Executive Council of Quebec">
        Executive Council of Quebec
       </a>
       is appointed.
      </p>
      <p>
       Until 1968, the Quebec legislature was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicameral" title="Bicameral">
        bicameral
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
        <a href="#cite_note-120">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       consisting of the
       <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Council_of_Quebec" title="Legislative Council of Quebec">
        Legislative Council
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_of_Quebec" title="Legislative Assembly of Quebec">
        Legislative Assembly
       </a>
       . In that year, the Legislative Council was abolished and the Legislative Assembly was renamed the National Assembly. Quebec was the last province to abolish its legislative council.
      </p>
      <p>
       The government of Quebec awards an order of merit called the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Order_of_Quebec" title="National Order of Quebec">
        National Order of Quebec
       </a>
       . It is inspired in part by the French
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%C3%A9gion_d%27honneur" title="Légion d'honneur">
        Legion of Honour
       </a>
       . It is conferred upon men and women born or living in Quebec (but non-Quebecers can be inducted as well) for outstanding achievements.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE_121-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CE-121">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The government of Quebec takes the majority of its revenue through a
       <a href="/wiki/Progressive_tax" title="Progressive tax">
        progressive income tax
       </a>
       , a 9.975% sales tax
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
        <a href="#cite_note-122">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and various other taxes (such as carbon, corporate and capital gains taxes),
       <a href="/wiki/Equalization_payments_in_Canada" title="Equalization payments in Canada">
        equalization payments
       </a>
       from the federal government, transfer payments from other provinces and direct payments.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
        <a href="#cite_note-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By some measures Quebec is the highest taxed province;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
        <a href="#cite_note-124">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a 2012 study indicated that "Quebec companies pay 26 per cent more in taxes than the Canadian average".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
        <a href="#cite_note-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A 2014 report by the
       <a href="/wiki/Fraser_Institute" title="Fraser Institute">
        Fraser Institute
       </a>
       indicated that "Relative to its size, Quebec is the most indebted province in Canada by a wide margin".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
        <a href="#cite_note-126">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_subdivisions">
        Administrative subdivisions
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Administrative_subdivisions_of_Quebec" title="Administrative subdivisions of Quebec">
        Administrative subdivisions of Quebec
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Quebec has subdivisions at the regional, supralocal and local levels. Excluding administrative units reserved for Aboriginal lands, the primary types of subdivision are:
      </p>
      <p>
       At the regional level:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        17
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regions_of_Quebec" title="Regions of Quebec">
         administrative regions
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       At the supralocal level:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        86
        <a href="/wiki/Regional_county_municipality" title="Regional county municipality">
         regional county municipalities
        </a>
        or RCMs (
        <i>
         municipalités régionales de comté
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         MRC
        </i>
        );
       </li>
       <li>
        2
        <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Community_(Quebec)" title="Metropolitan Community (Quebec)">
         metropolitan communities
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         communautés métropolitaines
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       At the local level:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        1,117
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_government_in_Quebec" title="Local government in Quebec">
         local municipalities
        </a>
        of various
        <a href="/wiki/Types_of_municipalities_in_Quebec" title="Types of municipalities in Quebec">
         types
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        11
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_agglomerations_of_Quebec" title="Urban agglomerations of Quebec">
         agglomerations
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         agglomérations
        </i>
        ) grouping 42 of these local municipalities;
       </li>
       <li>
        within 8 local municipalities, 45
        <a href="/wiki/List_of_boroughs_in_Quebec" title="List of boroughs in Quebec">
         boroughs
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         arrondissements
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>

      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Canada_2016_Census" title="Canada 2016 Census">
        2016 census
       </a>
       , Quebec had a population of 8,164,361 living in 3,531,663 of its 3,858,943 total dwellings, a 3.3% change from its 2011 population of 7,903,001. With a land area of 1,356,625.27 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (523,795.95 sq mi), it had a population density of 6.0/km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (15.6/sq mi) in 2016.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StatCan2016_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-StatCan2016-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2013, Statistics Canada estimated the province's population to be 8,155,334.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estimates_129-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-estimates-129">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       At 1.69 children per woman, Quebec's 2011
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" title="Fertility rate">
        fertility rate
       </a>
       is above the Canada-wide rate of 1.61,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-radio-canada.ca_130-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-radio-canada.ca-130">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and is higher than it was at the turn of the 21st century. However, it is still below the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_rate#Related_parameters" title="Fertility rate">
        replacement fertility rate
       </a>
       of 2.1. This contrasts with its fertility rates before 1960, which were among the highest of any industrialized society. Although Quebec is home to only 24% of the population of Canada, the number of international adoptions in Quebec is the highest of all provinces of Canada. In 2001, 42% of international adoptions in Canada were carried out in Quebec. By 2012, the population of Quebec reached 8 million, and it is projected to reach 9.2 million in 2056.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
        <a href="#cite_note-131">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Life expectancy in Quebec reached a new high in 2011, with an expectancy of 78.6 years for men and 83.2 years for women; this ranked as the third-longest life expectancy among Canadian provinces, behind those of
       <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">
        British Columbia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">
        Ontario
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-radio-canada.ca_130-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-radio-canada.ca-130">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       All the tables in the following section have been reduced from their original size, for full tables see main article
       <i>
        Demographics of Quebec
       </i>
       .
      </p>

       Origins in this table are self-reported and respondents were allowed to give more than one answer.
      </p>

        <td>
         2%
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Canadian" title="German Canadian">
          German
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         131,795
        </td>
        <td>
         1.8%
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div style="clear:left;">
      </div>
      <p>
       <small>
        <i>
         Percentages are calculated as a proportion of the total number of respondents (7,435,905) and may total more than 100 percent due to dual responses.
         <br/>
         Only groups with 1.5 percent or more of respondents are shown.
        </i>
       </small>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
        <a href="#cite_note-132">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The 2006 census counted a total aboriginal population of 108,425 (1.5 percent) including 65,085
       <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">
        North American Indians
       </a>
       (0.9 percent), 27,985
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C3%A9tis_people_(Canada)" title="Métis people (Canada)">
        Métis
       </a>
       (0.4 percent), and 10,950
       <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">
        Inuit
       </a>
       (0.15 percent). It should be noted however, that there is a significant undercount, as many of the biggest Indian bands regularly refuse to participate in Canadian censuses for political reasons regarding the question of aboriginal sovereignty. In particular, the largest
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohawk_nation" title="Mohawk nation">
        Mohawk
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">
        Iroquois
       </a>
       reserves (
       <a href="/wiki/Kahnawake" title="Kahnawake">
        Kahnawake
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Akwesasne" title="Akwesasne">
        Akwesasne
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Kanesatake" title="Kanesatake">
        Kanesatake
       </a>
       ) were not counted.
      </p>
      <p>
       Nearly 9% of the population of Quebec belongs to a
       <a href="/wiki/Visible_minority" title="Visible minority">
        visible minority
       </a>
       group. This is a lower percentage than that of
       <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">
        British Columbia
       </a>
       , Ontario,
       <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">
        Alberta
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">
        Manitoba
       </a>
       but higher than that of the other five provinces. Most visible minorities in Quebec live in or near
       <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">
        Montreal
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PQuebecVM2006.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="639" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PQuebecVM2006.jpg/300px-PQuebecVM2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PQuebecVM2006.jpg/450px-PQuebecVM2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PQuebecVM2006.jpg/600px-PQuebecVM2006.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PQuebecVM2006.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Visible minorities in Quebec
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>

        </td>
